The Legal Effect of Nukul (Refusal to Swear) in Hanafi Law
Presenting Hanafi position on nukul with proof and responding to Shafi'i view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that nukul (defendant's refusal to swear) is a sufficient basis for judgment and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A defendant's refusal to swear (nukul) is taken as admission in Hanafi law, unlike in Shafi'i where it only shifts the oath to the plaintiff.

When a plaintiff has no witnesses, the defendant is typically asked to swear denial. But what if the defendant refuses to swear? This passage presents the Hanafi school's view that nukul alone justifies judgment against the defendant, and contrasts it with Imam al-Shafi'i's position that the oath should be returned to the plaintiff. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how each madhhab weighs probabilities in judicial proof.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that according to the Hanafi school, the judge rules against the defendant solely based on his refusal to take the oath (nukul). This is the ruling attributed to al-Quduri.
The ruling is unconditional: nukul itself is sufficient evidence.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that nukul is ambiguous—it could be due to piety or caution—so it cannot serve as proof. Instead, the oath should be returned to the plaintiff; if the plaintiff swears, judgment is for him.
The Shafi'i view places the burden on the plaintiff after nukul.
3
Analyse the Hanafi rational proof
The Hanafi dalil 'aqlī states that a truthful defendant would naturally swear to fulfill the obligation and repel harm. Refusal indicates either admission or willingness to pay unjustly. This inference is stronger than the mere possibility of piety.
The proof is based on human nature and legal obligation (wujūb al-yamīn).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in Hanafi law, the defendant loses immediately without further proceedings. In Shafi'i law, the plaintiff must take an oath to win; if he also refuses, the case is dismissed.
This difference affects court procedure and the burden of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قال واذا نكل المدعى عليه عن اليمين قضى عليه بالنكول والزمه ما ادعى عليه وقال الشافعي لا يقضى به بل يرد اليمين على المدعى فاذا حلف يقضى به لان النكول يحتمل التورع عن اليمين الكاذبة والترفع عن الصادقة واشتباه الحال فلا ينتصب حجة مع الاحتمال ويمين المدعى دليل الظهور فيصار اليه ولنا ان النكول دل على كونه باذلا او مقرا اذ لو لا ذالك لاقدم على اليمين اقامة للواجب ودفعا للضرر عن نفسه فيترجح هذا الجانب ولا وجه لرد اليمين على المدعى لما قدمناه ترجمہ اور جب مدعی علیہ نے قسم سے نکار کیا تو قاضی انکار کی وجہ سے اس کے خلاف فیصلہ کرے گا اور جو کچھ مدعی نے اس پر دعوی کیا ہے وہ اس کے ذمہ لازم کرے گا اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ قاضی انکار کی وجہ سے مدعی علیہ پر حکم نہ دے گا بلکہ قسم کو مدعی پر لوٹائے گا پس اگر مدعی قسم کھا گیا تو قاضی اس کے واسطے حکم دیدے گا کیونکہ مدعی علیہ کا قسم سے انکار کرنا اس بات کا احتمال رکھتا ہے کہ مدعی علیہ جھوٹی قسم سے پر ہیز کرنا چاہتا ہے اور کچی قسم ہے احتیاط کرنا چاہتا ہے۔ (بہر حال ) مدعی علیہ کا انکار اشتباہ حال کا احتمال رکھتا ہے لہذا یہ انکا ر اس احتمال کے ساتھ حجت نہیں ہو سکتا ہے اور مدعی کا قسم کھانا ظہور حق کی دلیل ہے۔ لہذا اسی کی طرف رجوع کیا جائے گا۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے مدعی علیہ کا قسم سے انکار اس بات پر دلالت کرتا ہے کہ مدعی علیہ مدعی بہ کو دلیری کے ساتھ دینا چاہتا ہے یا وہ مدعی کے دعوی کا معترف ہے۔
اس لئے کہ اگر ایسانہ ہوتا تو تم کھانے پر اقدام کرتا تا کہ جو کچھ شریعت نے واجب کیا ہے وہ ادا کرے اور اپنے اوپر سے ضرر کو دور کرے۔ لہذا یہ جانب راجح ہوگی اور مدعی پر تم لوٹانے کی کوئی وجہ نہیں ہے جیسا کہ ہم نے پہلے بیان کیا ہے۔ تشریح صاحب قدوری نے کہا کہ مدعی کے بینہ پیش نہ کرنے کی صورت میں اگر مدعی علیہ نے قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا تو مدعی علیہ کے انکار قسم کی وجہ سے قاضی مدعی علیہ کے خلاف حکم دے گا اور مدعی نے جو کچھ اس پر دعوی کیا ہے قاضی اس کو مدعی علیہ کے ذمہ لازم کرے گا۔
حضرت امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ مدعی علیہ کے انکار کرنے پر قاضی فیصلہ دینے کا مجاز نہیں ہے بلکہ قسم مدعی پر لوٹائی جائے گی ۔ چنا نچہ اگر مدعی قسم کھا گیا تو قاضی اس کے حق میں حکم صادر کر دے گا۔ اور اگر مدعی نے بھی انکار کر دیا تو مقدمہ خارج ہو جائے گا۔ بہر حال مدعی کے قسم کھانے کی صورت میں اس کے حق میں فیصلہ اس لئے صادر کیا جائے گا کہ مدعی علیہ کا قسم سے انکار کرنا اس بات کا احتمال رکھتا ہے کہ شاید مدعی علیہ جھوٹی قسم سے پر ہیز کرنا چاہتا ہو یا کچی قسم سے احتیاط کرنا چاہتا ہو الغرض مدعی علیہ کا حال مشتبہ ہو گیا اور اس احتمال اور اشتباہ حال کے ساتھ مدعی علیہ کا انکار مدعی کے حق میں حجت واقع نہیں ہو سکتا ہے اور مدعی کا قسم کھا نا مدعی کے حق پر ہونے کی بین دلیل ہے لہذا مدعی کی قسم کی طرف رجوع کیا جائے گا اور قاضی مدعی سے قسم کا مطالبہ کرے گا۔
ہماری دلیل ہ یہ ہے مدعی علیہ کا قسم کھانے سے انکار کرنا اس بات پر دلالت کرتا ہے کہ مدعی علیہ مدعی بہ کو دلیری اور جرات کے ساتھ دینا چاہتا ہے یعنی یہ کہتا ہے کہ مدعی کا دعویٰ تو غلط ہے لیکن میں قسم نہ کھا کر مدعی بہ ادا کر دوں گا یعنی ناحق طور پر ادا کرنا گوارا ہے لیکن قسم کھانا منظور نہیں ہے۔ یا مدعی علیہ اس کے دعوی کا اقرار کرتا ہے یعنی یہ کہتا ہے کہ مدعی کا دعوی درست ہے لہذا میں جھوٹی قسم نہ کھاؤں گا۔ وجہ اس کی یہ ہے کہ اگر ان میں سے کوئی بات نہ ہوئی تو وہ شریعت کی واجب کردہ تم کو ادا کرنے کے لئے اور اپنے اپر سے ضرر کو دور کرنے کے لئے قسم کھانے کا اقدام ضرور کرتا۔ پس اس کا قسم کھانے سے انکار کرنا اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ وہ مدعی کے دعوی کا اقرار کرتا ہے یا تم کو چھوڑ کر دلیری سے مدعی بہ ادا کرنا چاہتا ہے۔ لہذا امام شافعی کے بیان کردہ افعال کے مقابلہ میں اسی احتمال کو ترجیح حاصل ہوگی اور مدعی علیہ پر مدعی بہ کا حکم کر دیا جائے گا اور جب اس احتمال کو ترجیح حاصل ہے تو سابقہ دلیل ( کہ بعینہ مدعی پر ہے اور یمین منکر پر) کی وجہ سے مدعی کی طرف قسم پھیرنے کی کوئی وجہ نہیں ہے۔ قاضی مدعی علیہ سے کہے کہ میں تین دفعہ قسم پیش کرتا ہوں اگر وہ رد کر دے تو اس کے خلاف فیصلہ صادر کر دے
قال وينبغى للقاضى ان يقول له انى اعرض علیک الیمین ثلثا فان حلفت والافقضيت عليك بما ادعاه وهذا الانذار لاعلامه بالحكم اذهو موضع الخفاء . قال فاذا كرر العرض عليه ثلث مرات قضى عليه بالنكول ولهذا التكرار ذكره الخصاف لزيادة الاحتياط والمبالغة فى ايلاء العذر فإما المذهب انه لو قضى بالنكول بعد العرض مرة جاز لما قدمناه هو الصحيح والاول اولى ثم النكول قد يكون حقيقيا
If the defendant refuses the oath, then the judge shall rule against him due to the refusal and impose what the plaintiff claimed upon him. Al-Shafi'i said: He should not rule by it, but rather return the oath to the plaintiff; then if he swears, judgment is given by it, because refusal may be due to refraining from false oath or abstaining from a true oath, and the situation is ambiguous, so it cannot stand as proof with probability. And the plaintiff's oath is evidence of apparent truth, so one should resort to it. Our proof is that refusal indicates that he is either willing to pay or admitting; otherwise, he would have proceeded to take the oath to fulfill the obligation and repel harm from himself. So this aspect is preponderant, and there is no ground to return the oath to the plaintiff as we have stated. Translation: When the defendant refuses the oath, the judge will rule against him due to the refusal and impose what the plaintiff claimed upon him. Imam Shafi'i said: The judge should not rule against the defendant due to the refusal, but rather return the oath to the plaintiff; then if the plaintiff swears, the judge will give judgment for him. Because the defendant's refusal to swear may mean he wants to avoid a false oath or is cautious about a true oath. In any case, the defendant's refusal carries the possibility of ambiguity, so this refusal cannot be a proof with this possibility, and the plaintiff's oath is evidence of the appearance of truth, so one should resort to it. Our proof is that the defendant's refusal to swear indicates that he is willing to give the claimed thing audaciously or he is admitting the plaintiff's claim. Because if it were not so, he would proceed to swear to fulfill what the Shari'ah has made obligatory and to remove harm from himself. Therefore this aspect is preponderant, and there is no reason to return the oath to the plaintiff as we have stated. Explanation: Sahib al-Quduri said: When the plaintiff does not present evidence, if the defendant refuses to swear, the judge shall rule against the defendant due to his refusal and impose the claim upon him. Imam Shafi'i said: The judge is not authorized to rule due to the defendant's refusal; rather, the oath is returned to the plaintiff. If the plaintiff swears, the judge will issue judgment for him. If the plaintiff also refuses, the case is dismissed. In any case, when the plaintiff swears, judgment is given in his favor because the defendant's refusal to swear may be due to avoiding false oath or caution against a true oath; in short, the defendant's situation becomes ambiguous, and with this probability and ambiguity, the defendant's refusal cannot be a proof for the plaintiff, and the plaintiff's oath is clear evidence that he is in the right, so one should resort to the plaintiff's oath and the judge will demand the oath from the plaintiff. Our proof is that the defendant's refusal to swear indicates that he wants to give the claimed thing audaciously and bravely, i.e., he says the plaintiff's claim is false but I will give the claim without swearing, i.e., he is willing to pay unjustly but does not agree to swear. Or the defendant admits the claim, i.e., he says the plaintiff's claim is correct, so I will not swear falsely. The reason is that if neither of these were the case, he would definitely proceed to swear to fulfill the Shari'ah obligation and remove harm from himself. So his refusal to swear is proof that he admits the plaintiff's claim or wants to give the claim audaciously without swearing. Therefore, compared to the possibilities mentioned by Imam Shafi'i, this probability is preponderant, and judgment for the claim will be passed against the defendant. And when this probability is preponderant, there is no reason to revert the oath to the plaintiff due to the previous proof (that the oath is upon the one who denies). The judge should say to the defendant: I present the oath to you three times; if he refuses, the judge issues judgment against him. He said: It is appropriate for the judge to say to him: I present the oath to you three times; if you swear, good; otherwise, I will rule against you with what he claimed. This warning is to inform him of the ruling because it is a hidden matter. He said: When he repeats the presentation three times, he rules against him due to nukul. Al-Khassaf mentioned this repetition for extra caution and to give full excuse. As for the madhhab, if he rules after one presentation, it is permissible, as we mentioned; it is correct, and the first is preferable. Then nukul may be real.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the legal effect of the defendant's refusal to take an oath (nukul) when the plaintiff has no evidence. The Hanafi position, attributed to al-Quduri, is that the judge shall rule against the defendant solely based on his refusal (nukul). The Hanafi proof is rational: if the defendant were truthful, he would naturally swear to avoid liability; his refusal indicates either an admission of the claim or a willingness to pay unjustly. Thus, nukul is taken as evidence against him. Imam Shafi'i, on the other hand, argues that nukul is ambiguous—it could be due to piety or caution—so the oath should be returned to the plaintiff; if the plaintiff swears, judgment is for him; if he also refuses, the case is dismissed. The Hanafi response emphasizes that the stronger inference is that nukul indicates admission or defiance, and therefore there is no need to revert the oath to the plaintiff. The text also discusses procedural details: the judge should present the oath three times, though the relied-upon position (correct in the madhhab) is that one presentation suffices. The repetition is mentioned for caution, following al-Khassaf.

Key Terms
نکول (nukūl): The defendant's refusal to take the required oath after the plaintiff has no evidence.
یمین (yamīn): Oath taken in court, usually by the defendant in denial of the claim.
مدعی (mudda'ī): The plaintiff who brings a claim against the defendant.
مدعی علیہ (mudda'ā 'alayhi): The defendant against whom the claim is made.
قضاء (qaḍā): Judgment or court decision.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a defendant refuses to take the oath when the plaintiff has no witnesses, what should the judge do?
A) Require the plaintiff to take an oath
B) Dismiss the case due to lack of evidence
C) Rule against the defendant based on his refusal
D) Adjourn the case indefinitely

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a defendant's refusal to swear (nukul) is treated as sufficient evidence to give judgment against him, based on the rational inference that a truthful person would swear to avoid harm.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
12 min
The Ruling on Nukul and the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i on the legal effect of nukul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nukul, explain the rational proof (dalil aqli), compare it with the Shafi'i position, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
The defendant's refusal to swear is stronger evidence than the plaintiff's oath — it reveals admission or willingness to pay.

In a court case where the plaintiff has no evidence, what happens if the defendant refuses to take the oath? This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi school rules against the defendant immediately based on rational inference, while the Shafi'i school returns the oath to the plaintiff. We will analyze the proofs and the procedural details.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two positions
Understand the factual setting: plaintiff has no evidence, defendant refuses oath. Note the Hanafi ruling (judge rules against defendant) and Shafi'i ruling (oath returns to plaintiff).
The Shafi'i view is based on doubt created by refusal.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof and Shafi'i counter-proof
The Hanafi proof is rational (istidlal): a truthful defendant would swear to avoid liability; refusal indicates admission or willingness to pay. The Shafi'i counter-proof argues that refusal creates doubt, so the plaintiff's oath is more decisive.
The Hanafi proof treats refusal as a strong qarinah (circumstantial evidence).
3
Evaluate the Hanafi response and determine the practical consequence
The Hanafi response gives preponderance (tarjih) to the inference of admission, as the defendant's refusal is more telling. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): in a Hanafi court, immediate judgment against defendant; in a Shafi'i court, the plaintiff must swear to win.
This affects how judges in different madhhabs proceed.
4
Understand the procedural details: three presentations vs. one, and types of nukul
The judge warns the defendant and presents the oath three times (recommended) but once is legally sufficient. Nukul can be real (explicit refusal) or constructive (silence with no excuse).
The threefold presentation is mustahabb, not wajib, based on al-Hidayah.

كقوله لا احلف وقد يكون حكميا بان يسكت وحكمه حكم الاول اذا علم انه لاافة به من طرش او خرس
هو الصحيح
اور قاضی کے لئے مناسب ہے کہ وہ مدعی علیہ سے یوں ہے کہ میں تجھ پر تین بار تم پیش کرتا ہوں پس اگر تو نے قسم کھائی تو یہا ور نہ تو میں تجھ پر اس چیز کا فیصلہ کروں گا جس کا مدعی نے دعوی کیا ہے اور یہ ڈرانا اس کو انکار قسم کے حکم کی خبر دینے کے لئے ہے کیونکہ یہ پوشیدن کا مقام تے۔ میں جب قاضی نے مدعی علیہ پر تین بار قسم پیش کی تو انکار کی وجہ سے اس کے خلاف حکم کرے گا اور تین بار قسم کا پیش کرنا خصاف نے ذکر کیا ہے کیونکہ اس میں زیادہ احتیاط اور اظہار عذر میں مبالغہ ہے۔ بہر حال مذہب تو یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک بار پیش کرنے کے بعد انکار کیوجہ سے قاش نے حکم دید یا تو بھی جائز ہے، اس میں کی وجہ سے ہو ہم نے پہلے ان ان بے بیج سے اور مال اعمل سے ہو اوکا بھی حقیقی ہوتا ہے ہی اس کا قول میں قسم نہیں کھاؤں گا اور بھی کی بہتات ہے میں خور یہ اسکات سرے ، اس کا معمربی والے کے ضلم
کے مانند ہے بشرطیکہ یہ بات معلوم ، بہائے کہ مٹی مالیہ کو بہرے یا گونگے ہونے کی کو ئی آفت نہیں تھی۔ یہی نواب صحیح ہے۔ شریح صاحب قدوری بہتے ہیں کہ اگر مدنی کے پاس بعینہ نہ ہو اور مدنی ۔ قسم کے لئے تیار ہو گیا تو قاضی مدنی عایدت یہ کہے میں تجھے پر تین بار قسم پیش کر دوں گا اس توب قسم کھالی تو فبہا یعنی تو جھوٹی مدعی سے چھٹکارا پالے گا اور اگر تو نے قسم نہ کھائی بلکہ قسم کھانے سے ان مرد یا تو میں تیرے خلاف مدعی کے دعوی کا حکم کر دوں گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ نے کہا کہ قاضی کا یہ ڈرانا یعنی مدعی علیہ سے یہ کہنا کہ انکار کی صورت میں تیرے خلاف فیصلہ کروں گا اس غرض سے ہے کہ اس کو قسم سے انکار کا حکم بتلا دیا جائے کیونکہ یہ پوشیدگی کا مقام ہے یعنی ممکن ہے کہ مدلی علیہ کو امام شافعی کا مذہب معلوم ہو کہ انکار قسم سے مدی عالیہ پر کم نہیں ہوتا ہے بلکہ مودی پر قتم لوٹائی جاتی ہے پس قاضی اس کو اپنے مذہب سے ( کہ مدنی علیہ کے انکار کے بعد اس کے خلاف حکم کر دیا جائے گا ) باخبر کرو تا کہ مدنی علیہ پر یہ بات مخفی نہ ر ہے۔
صاحب قدوری نے کہا کہ جب قاضی مدعی علیہ پر تین بار تم پیش کر چکا او مدنی علیہ ہر بار انکار کر چکا تو اب قاضی اس کے خلاف حکم کرے گا صاحب ہدایہ کہتے ہیں کہ یہ تکرار یعنی تین بار قسم کو پیش کرنا امام صاحب کے بیان کے مطابق استحباب کے درجہ میں ہے۔ شرط اور واجب کے درجہ میں نہیں ہے اور اس کا مقصد زیادہ احتیاط اور اظہار عذر میں مبالغہ ہے جیسا کہ مرتد کو تین دن کی مہلت دی جاتی ہے۔ پس یہ تین دن کی مہلت مستحب ہے نہ کہ واجب بہر حال تین بار قسم کا پیش کرنا احتیاط کی وجہ سے ہے۔ ورنہ تو مذہب مفتی یہ حکم یہ ہے کہ اگر قاضی نے ایک بار قسم پیش کرنے کے بعد مدعلی علیہ کے انکار کی بنیاد پر حکم دیدیا تو بھی جائز ہے۔ اور دلیل سابق میں گذر چکی کہ مدعلی علیہ انکار کر کے مدنی بہ کو دلیری کے ساتھ دینا چاہتا ہے یا مدگی کے دعوئی کا اقرار کرتا ہے اور ان دونوں میں سے کسی میں تکرار شریر نہیں ہے لہذا قسم پیش کرنے میں بھی تکرار شرط نہ ہو گا صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہی قول صحیح ہے یعنی مدعی علیہ کے ایک بارا انکار کے بعد اگر قاضی نے فیصلہ کر دیا تو یہ فیصلہ نافذ ہو جائے گا۔
اگر چہ بعض لوگوں نے کہا کہ ایک بارانکار کے بعد اگر قاضی نے حکم کیا تو یہ حکم نافذ ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ کہتے ہیں کہ اول اولی ہے یعنی ایک بار انکار کے بعد فیصلہ کرنا اگر چہ جائز ہے لیکن بہتر یہ ہے کہ تین بار قسم پیش کرنے
He is acknowledging the claim. Because if it were not so, he would have proceeded to take the oath to fulfill what the Shari'ah has made obligatory and to remove harm from himself. Therefore this probability will be preponderant, and there is no reason to return the oath to the plaintiff, as we have explained earlier. Explanation: Sahib al-Quduri said: If the plaintiff does not present evidence and the defendant refuses to take the oath, then due to the defendant's refusal of the oath, the judge will give a ruling against the defendant and will make binding upon the defendant what the plaintiff claimed. Imam al-Shafi'i said: The judge is not authorized to give a ruling upon the defendant's refusal; rather the oath will be returned to the plaintiff. Then if the plaintiff swears, the judge will issue a ruling in his favor. And if the plaintiff also refuses, the case will be dismissed. In any case, the ruling in favor of the plaintiff if he swears is because the defendant's refusal to swear indicates that perhaps the defendant wants to avoid a false oath or is cautious about a harsh oath; in short, the defendant's situation becomes doubtful, and with this doubt, the defendant's refusal cannot be evidence against the plaintiff. The plaintiff's swearing is a clear proof that he is in the right, so recourse is made to the plaintiff's oath, and the judge will demand the oath from the plaintiff. Our proof is: The defendant's refusal to swear indicates that the defendant wants to give the claimed property with boldness and audacity, i.e., he says: 'The plaintiff's claim is false, but I will give the claimed property without swearing, meaning I tolerate giving it wrongfully but do not approve of swearing.' Or the defendant admits the claim, i.e., he says: 'The plaintiff's claim is correct, so I will not swear falsely.' The reason is: If neither of these were the case, he would certainly proceed to take the oath to fulfill what the Shari'ah has made obligatory and to remove harm from himself. So his refusal to swear is proof that he either admits the claim or wants to give the claimed property with boldness instead of swearing. Therefore, this probability is given preference over the probabilities mentioned by Imam al-Shafi'i, and the claimed property is adjudged against the defendant. And since this probability is preponderant, there is no reason to return the oath to the plaintiff based on the earlier proof (that the oath is upon the defendant). The judge should say to the defendant: 'I present the oath to you three times; if he refuses, he should issue a ruling against him.' He said: It is appropriate for the judge to say to him: 'I present the oath to you three times. If you swear, [good], otherwise I will rule against you for what he claimed.' This warning is to inform him of the ruling because it is a place of obscurity. He said: When he repeats the presentation three times, he rules against him due to nukul. Al-Khassaf mentioned this repetition for extra caution and exaggeration in granting excuse. As for the madhhab, if he ruled due to nukul after presenting once, it is permissible, as we have stated; that is correct, and the first is better. Then nukul may be real, like his saying 'I will not swear,' and may be constructive (hukmi) such as silence; its ruling is like the first when it is known that he has no impediment like deafness or muteness. That is correct. And it is appropriate for the judge to say to him: 'I present the oath to you three times. If you swear, then fine; otherwise I will rule against you for what the plaintiff claimed.' This warning is to inform him of the ruling of refusal because it is a place of obscurity. When the judge presents the oath three times to the defendant, then due to refusal he will rule against him. Al-Khassaf mentioned presenting the oath three times because it involves extra caution and exaggeration in granting excuse. Nevertheless, the madhhab is that if after presenting once, due to refusal, the judge gave a ruling, it is also permissible; the proof of this is what we have mentioned earlier. Then nukul may be real, like his statement 'I will not swear,' and may be constructive like silence; its ruling is like the first provided it is known that he has no affliction like deafness or muteness. That is the correct view. Sahib al-Quduri says: If the plaintiff does not have evidence and the defendant becomes ready for the oath, then the judge says to the defendant: 'I will present the oath to you three times. If you swear, then fine (i.e., you will be free from the plaintiff), and if you do not swear but refuse, then I will rule against you on the plaintiff's claim.' Sahib al-Hidayah said: The judge's warning (i.e., telling the defendant that in case of refusal he will rule against him) is for the purpose of informing him of the ruling of refusal of oath because it is a place of obscurity. That is, it is possible that the defendant knows the madhhab of Imam al-Shafi'i that refusal of oath does not result in a ruling against the defendant but rather the oath is returned to the plaintiff. So the judge informs him of his own madhhab (that after the defendant's refusal, a ruling will be given against him) so that this matter does not remain hidden from the defendant. Sahib al-Quduri said: When the judge has presented the oath three times to the defendant and the defendant has refused each time, then the judge will rule against him. Sahib al-Hidayah says: This repetition (i.e., presenting the oath three times) is in the category of istihbab (recommendation) according to the statement of Imam (Abu Hanifa), not in the category of condition or obligation. Its purpose is extra caution and exaggeration in granting excuse, just as an apostate is given a three-day respite. So this three-day respite is mustahabb, not wajib. In any case, presenting the oath three times is due to caution. Otherwise, the operative madhhab (al-mufta bihi) is that if the judge gave a ruling after presenting once based on the defendant's refusal, it is also permissible. The proof has passed earlier that the defendant by refusing either wants to give the claimed property with boldness or admits the claim, and in neither of these is repetition a condition. Therefore, repetition is not a condition in presenting the oath either. Sahib al-Hidayah says that this is the correct saying, i.e., if the judge gave a ruling after the defendant's refusal once, that ruling will be effective. Although some people said that if the judge gave a ruling after one refusal, it would be effective. Sahib al-Hidayah says that the first (i.e., three times) is better, i.e., ruling after one refusal is permissible, but it is better to present the oath three times.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses the ruling on the defendant's refusal to take an oath (nukul) when the plaintiff lacks evidence. It is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i. The passage begins by explaining the Hanafi position: when the plaintiff has no evidence and the defendant refuses to swear, the judge rules against the defendant. The reasoning is rational (istidlal bil-aqli): the defendant's refusal indicates either that he admits the claim or that he is willing to pay the claimed amount without swearing out of audacity. In either case, it is against him. Imam al-Shafi'i's view is that the oath should be offered to the plaintiff; if the plaintiff swears, judgment is for him; if he refuses, the case is dismissed. The Shafi'i reasoning is that the defendant's refusal creates doubt, so the oath is transferred to the plaintiff as a stronger proof. The Hanafi rebuttal argues that the defendant's refusal is itself a strong indication (qarinah) that he is liable, so it is treated as an admission or willingness to pay. This probability is given preponderance (tarjih) over the Shafi'i interpretation. Hence, the oath is not returned to the plaintiff. The passage then discusses the procedure: the judge should warn the defendant of the consequence of refusal and present the oath three times. The threefold presentation is mentioned by al-Khassaf for extra caution, but the madhhab is that one presentation is sufficient; however, three is better (istihbab). The passage concludes with the ruling that nukul can be real (explicit refusal) or constructive (silence without valid excuse), and the same applies.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukul): The refusal of the defendant to take the oath after being requested by the judge.
الیمین (al-yamin): The oath administered by the judge to the defendant (or plaintiff in some cases) to establish the truth of a claim.
المدعی (al-mudda'i): The plaintiff who brings a claim against another person.
المدعی علیہ (al-mudda'a 'alayh): The defendant against whom a claim is made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the consequence when the defendant refuses to take the oath after the plaintiff fails to produce evidence?
A) The judge returns the oath to the plaintiff.
B) The case is dismissed immediately.
C) The judge rules against the defendant based on his refusal.
D) The defendant is imprisoned until he swears.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats the defendant's refusal to swear as decisive evidence of liability, leading to immediate judgment, unlike the Shafi'i school which returns the oath to the plaintiff.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
6 min
Judge's Procedure in Offering Oath and Ruling on Refusal
Explaining the number of times to offer oath and the effect of refusal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the number of oath presentations required and explain the effect of the defendant's refusal (nukul) on the judge's judgment.

Core Idea
One refusal is enough to pass judgment; three presentations are recommended but not required.

Is the judge required to offer the oath three times before ruling against a defendant who refuses? This passage from Kitab al-Da'wa clarifies the Hanafi position: a single refusal suffices, though three presentations are recommended for caution. It also distinguishes between explicit refusal and constructive refusal by silence, and explains the proof behind the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish real vs constructive refusal
Identify that refusal can be explicit (saying 'I will not swear') or constructive (silence after the oath is offered). Both are treated the same, provided silence is not due to deafness or muteness.
Constructive refusal is called nukul hukmi.
2
Understand the judge's warning announcement
The judge should warn the defendant: 'I present you the oath three times; if you swear, good; otherwise, I will rule against you.' This warning informs the defendant of the consequence of refusal.
The mention of three times is from Khasaf for caution.
3
Identify the correct Hanafi position on number of presentations
The madhhab (correct view) is that one presentation suffices; judgment after one refusal is valid and effective. Three is mustahab (recommended) but not required. Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah declares this as sahih.
The proof: refusal is treated as admission or voluntary payment (badhl), which doesn't require repetition.
4
Apply the ruling to constructive refusal
If the defendant remains silent, the judge must first confirm he is not deaf or mute (by inquiry). If clear that he is not, the silence is considered a refusal and judgment can be passed.
Some scholars said to imprison him until he responds, but the preferred view is to rely on inquiry.

اول قول صحیح ہے۔
Like his saying 'I will not swear' and it may be constructive by remaining silent, and its ruling is the same as the first when it is known that he has no defect like deafness or muteness; that is correct. And it is appropriate for the judge to say to the defendant: 'I present you the oath three times; if you swear, then good; if not, I will pass judgment against you for what the plaintiff claimed.' This warning is to inform him of the ruling of refusal to swear because this is a place of concealment. When the judge presents the oath three times and the defendant refuses, he will pass judgment against him. Mentioning three times is from Khasaf because it entails more caution and exaggeration in showing excuse. Nevertheless, the madhhab is that if after one presentation the defendant refuses and the judge passes judgment, it is permissible. Its proof is what has passed: the defendant by refusal intends to give the plaintiff the money boldly or admits the claim, and neither requires repetition. Therefore, repetition in presenting the oath is not a condition. The author of al-Hidayah says that this is the correct view: after one refusal, if the judge passes judgment, it is effective. Although some said that after one refusal the judgment is not effective. The author says the first is preferable: it is permissible but better to pass judgment after three presentations. The commentator says refusal may be real, e.g., after the judge presents the oath the defendant says 'I will not swear,' or constructive, e.g., he remains silent. The author says constructive refusal has the same ruling as real refusal, provided it is known that the defendant is not deaf or mute due to a divine affliction. That is correct: after presenting the oath, if the defendant remains silent, some think that people should be inquired about whether he is deaf or mute; if they say no, the judge considers him a refuser and passes judgment against him. Others say he should be imprisoned until he responds. The first view is correct.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Da'wa explains the judge's procedure in presenting the oath to the defendant and the effect of refusal (nukul). It combines the Arabic matn and Urdu sharh. The text first distinguishes between real refusal (explicit statement 'I will not swear') and constructive refusal (silence after the oath is offered). The ruling for both is the same: the judge may pass judgment against the defendant, provided the silence is not due to deafness or muteness. The judge should warn the defendant by saying 'I present you the oath three times; if you swear, good; otherwise, I will rule against you.' This warning is to inform the defendant that refusal will lead to a judgment, especially because some people might be unaware of the Hanafi ruling on nukul. The number three is mentioned by Khasaf for caution, but the correct Hanafi position is that one presentation and one refusal suffice for the judge to pass a valid judgment. The proof is that refusal is considered either an admission of the claim or a voluntary payment (badhl), and neither action requires repetition to be effective. Therefore, the judge's judgment after one refusal is valid and enforceable. However, it is recommended (mustahab) to offer the oath three times to exercise extra caution. The commentator also notes that if the defendant remains silent, some scholars suggested inquiring from people about his hearing/speech ability, while others said to imprison him until he responds. The text prefers the first view: if it is known he is not deaf/mute, his silence is considered a constructive refusal.

Key Terms
نكول (nukūl): Refusal to swear an oath after the judge presents it; treated as an admission of the claim or a voluntary payment.
حكمي (ḥukmī): Constructive; here refers to refusal by silence rather than explicit words.
باذل (bādhil): One who gives something willingly; here the defendant who refuses the oath is considered as paying the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, after how many refusals of the oath can the judge pass judgment against the defendant?
A) Three refusals are required.
B) One refusal is sufficient, and judgment is effective.
C) The judge must offer the oath seven times.
D) The judge cannot pass judgment based on refusal at all.

Key Takeaway
A single refusal of the oath suffices for a valid judgment; the three-time presentation is only recommended for caution.

Oath in Non-Property Claims: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Nukul
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath administration in non-property claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the administration of oaths in claims of marriage, lineage, and similar matters, and apply the concepts of badhl and iqrar to distinguish property claims from non-property claims.

Core Idea
The defendant's refusal to swear is treated as payment only in property claims — in personal status claims, it is not equivalent to admission.

In property disputes, if the defendant refuses to swear, the judge can rule against him. But what about claims like marriage or lineage? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples disagreed on whether the same rule applies. This passage from al-Hidayah lays out their respective proofs based on the legal concepts of badhl (voluntary payment) and iqrar (admission). Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify the scope of legal presumptions in Hanafi qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claims in Dispute
List the non-property claims where the defendant's oath is debated: marriage (nikah), revocation (raj'a), return in ila', slavery, istilad, lineage, wala', hudud, and li'an.
These are all matters of personal status or punishments, not financial.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and His Dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that no oath is administered in any of these claims. His proof: nukul (refusal to swear) is treated as badhl (voluntary payment), not as admission. Badhl only applies to property claims, so the oath is not administered here.
Badhl means treating the defendant's refusal as if he has given up the claim, thus the oath drops.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Their Dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the oath is administered in all these claims except hudud and li'an. Their proof: nukul is equivalent to an admission (iqrar) or a substitute for it, and admission runs in all matters. However, hudud and li'an are exceptions because they are repelled by shubuhat (suspicions).
The Sahibayn consider nukul as strong evidence of the truth of the claim.
4
Analyze the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: In a marriage claim, if the defendant refuses the oath, Abu Hanifa's view does not allow the judge to rule against him on that basis; the Sahibayn's view allows it except in hudud/li'an.
This affects whether the judge can decide the case without further evidence.
5
Note the Exception for Hudud and Li'an
Both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree that no oath is administered in hudud and li'an due to shubuhat. This is a point of consensus despite the broader ikhtilaf.
Hudud are repelled by doubts, and li'an is in the meaning of hudud.

And if the claim is marriage, the defendant is not administered the oath according to Abu Hanifa. And according to him, no oath is administered in cases of marriage, revocation (raj'a), return in ila', slavery, istilad, lineage, wala', hudud, and li'an. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: oath is administered in all of these except hudud and li'an. The form of istilad is that the slave woman says, 'I am the umm walad of my master, and this is my son from him,' and the master denies it. Because if the master claims, istilad is established by his admission, and her denial is not considered. Their (the two) proof is that nukul (refusal to swear) is an admission because it indicates that the defendant is lying in his denial, as we have explained previously, so it is an admission or a substitute for it, and admission runs in these matters. But it is an admission with shubha (suspicion), and hudud are repelled by shubuhat, and li'an is in the meaning of hudud. Abu Hanifa's proof is that it is badhl (voluntary payment), because with it the oath does not remain wajib (obligatory) since the objective is obtained, and treating him as a badhil (payer) is better so that he does not become a liar in his denial. And badhl does not run in these matters. The benefit of administering the oath is to pass judgment based on nukul, so he is not administered the oath; rather this is badhl to repel litigation, which is owned by the mukatab and the authorized slave like moderate hospitality, and its validity in deeming (a claim) is based on the plaintiff's assertion, and he takes possession of it as a right for himself. And badhl here means leaving off prevention, and the matter of wealth is easy.
Translation: And if the claim is marriage, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the oath is not taken from the defendant. And according to Imam Abu Hanifa, in marriage, raj'a, return in ila', slavery, istilad, lineage, wala', hudud, li'an, the oath is not taken. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the oath will be taken in all of these except hudud and li'an. The form of istilad is that the slave woman says, 'I am the umm walad of my master, and this is my son born from him,' and the master denies it. Because if the master claimed, istilad would be established by his admission, and her denial would not be regarded. Their (the two) proof is that refusal to swear is an admission because the defendant's refusal to swear indicates that he is lying in his denial, as we have explained previously. Thus, refusal to swear is an admission of the claim or a substitute for admission, and admission runs in these matters. But it is an admission with shubha, and hudud are repelled by shubuhat, and li'an is in the meaning of hudud. And Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that refusal to swear is a badhl (voluntary payment), because with it the oath does not remain obligatory since the objective is obtained, and it is better to consider him a badhil so that he does not become a liar in his denial. And badhl does not run in these matters.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding whether the defendant should be administered an oath in claims other than property, specifically in nikah (marriage), raj'a (revocation of divorce), al-fay' fi al-ila' (return during ila'), riqq (slavery), istilad (claim of being umm walad), nasab (lineage), wala' (clientage), hudud (fixed punishments), and li'an (mutual imprecation). Abu Hanifa's position is that no oath is administered in any of these cases. The Sahibayn hold that the oath is administered in all of them except hudud and li'an. The example of istilad is given where a slave woman claims to be umm walad (mother of her master's child) and the master denies. If the master were to claim, istilad would be established by his own admission, so her denial is irrelevant. The Sahibayn's proof is that nukul (refusal to swear) is equivalent to an admission (iqrar) because it indicates the defendant is lying in his denial. Since admission is valid in all these matters, nukul should also be valid. However, they concede that it is an admission with shubha (doubt), and hudud and li'an are repelled by shubuhat, so nukul does not apply there. Abu Hanifa's proof is that in these matters, the defendant's refusal is better understood as badhl (voluntary payment) rather than an admission. When the defendant refuses the oath, the objective of the plaintiff is achieved (the defendant is effectively giving up the claim), so the oath is no longer required. Treating him as a payer (badhil) is preferable to avoid him becoming a liar. However, badhl only applies in property claims, not in the non-property matters listed. Therefore, nukul cannot be used as a basis for judgment in these cases. The practical difference is clear: according to Abu Hanifa, the judge cannot force the defendant to swear nor can he pass judgment based on nukul in these matters; according to the Sahibayn, he can except in hudud and li'an.

Key Terms
بذل (badhl): Voluntary payment or giving; here the defendant's refusal is considered as voluntarily giving the claimed property, thus the oath is dropped.
استيلاد (istīlād): The status of a slave woman becoming umm walad (mother of her master's child), giving her freedom upon the master's death.
نكول (nukūl): Refusal to swear an oath; treated either as admission (iqrar) or as badhl (payment) depending on the context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Marriage (nikah)
B) Lineage (nasab)
C) Fixed punishment (hudud)
D) Revocation (raj'a)

Key Takeaway
The classification of nukul as either badhl or iqrar determines whether the judge can rely on it in non-property claims, and this distinction splits the Hanafi school.

Constructive Denial (Inkār Hukmī) in Hanafi Qada
Explains constructive denial as equivalent to actual denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actual and constructive denial, and state the preferred Hanafi procedure when a defendant remains silent after oath tender.

Core Idea
Silence after oath is legally treated as denial unless the defendant is physically incapable — the judge must investigate first.

In Hanafi litigation, a defendant's response to an oath determines the course of judgment. This lesson examines how silence is legally construed as denial, and the differing opinions within the school on how a judge should proceed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of denial
Recognize the distinction between actual (haqīqī) denial—explicit refusal to take the oath—and constructive (hukmī) denial—silence after the oath is presented.
Constructive denial is the focus of this passage.
2
Understand the default ruling
The author of al-Hidayah states that constructive denial has the same legal effect as actual denial, provided the defendant is not physically incapacitated.
This equivalence is the baseline rule.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi scholars hold that the defendant should be imprisoned until he responds; others say the judge should first inquire about any physical impairment (deafness/muteness).
The dispute is about the procedure, not the underlying ruling.
4
Identify the preferred view
The commentator prefers the view that the judge should inquire among people. If no incapacity is found, silence is treated as denial and judgment is passed against the defendant.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ashraf al-Hidayah, Volume Ten, Book of Claims. After that, the judge issues a ruling. The commentator of al-Hidayah says: Denial is sometimes actual, e.g., after the judge presents the oath, the defendant says 'I will not take the oath.' And sometimes it is constructive (hakmi), e.g., after the judge presents the oath, the defendant remains silent. The author of al-Hidayah says: The ruling of constructive denial is the same as the ruling of actual denial. Provided that it becomes known that the defendant is not deaf or mute due to some divine calamity. This is correct. That is, after the oath is presented to the defendant, if the defendant remains silent, some scholars think that inquiries should be made among the people: is this defendant deaf or mute? If the people say he is not, then the judge will consider him a denier and pass a judgment against him. But some people say he should be imprisoned until he answers. The first opinion is correct.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah discusses the concept of constructive denial (inkār hakmi). It states that when the defendant remains silent after being offered the oath by the judge, this silence is treated as a denial, provided the defendant is not physically incapacitated (deaf or mute). There is a difference of opinion among Hanafi jurists: some say the judge should investigate the defendant's condition by asking people, while others say the defendant should be imprisoned until he responds. The author prefers the first view—that the judge should inquire and if no incapacity is found, treat the silence as denial and decide the case against the defendant.

Key Terms
انكار حقيقي (inkār haqīqī): Explicit verbal denial by the defendant after the oath is presented.
انكار حكمي (inkār hukmī): Constructive denial: silence of the defendant after the oath is tendered, which is legally treated as denial.
سكوت (sukūt): Silence of the defendant after the oath is offered by the judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi litigation, a defendant's silence after oath tender is considered constructive denial only after the judge confirms the defendant is not physically incapacitated.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Oath-Taking in Nine Claims
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath-taking in nine non-financial claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nine cases where Abu Hanifa does not swear the defendant, explain the Sahibayn's disagreement, and apply the underlying principles of nukul-as-iqrar and badhl to determine the applicable ruling in given scenarios.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa treats refusal to swear as payment (badhl) – not applicable in personal status; the Sahibayn treat it as acknowledgment – applicable except in hudud/li'an.

The Hanafi school's procedural rules on oath-taking (istihlaf) in litigation reveal a deep intra-madhhab ikhtilaf. While hudud and li'an are universally excluded, seven other claims – marriage, raj'a, ila', slavery, istilad, lineage, and wala – spark disagreement. This lesson unpacks the two competing theories: nukul as acknowledgment (iqrar) versus nukul as payment (badhl), and how they shape judicial practice.

Guided Path
1
List the nine cases
Identify from the text the nine claims where Abu Hanifa rules no oath is taken: marriage, raj'a, fay' in ila', riqq, istilad, nasab, wala, hudud, and li'an.
Hudud and li'an are agreed upon; the remaining seven are disputed.
2
Contrast Sahibayn's position
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that oath is taken in all except hudud and li'an. Recognize that the disagreement covers seven of the nine cases.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's dalil
Understand Abu Hanifa's argument: refusal to swear (nukul) is treated as badhl (payment to end litigation). Badhl is a transfer of property, valid only in financial claims. Since these cases involve personal status or God's rights, badhl cannot apply, so no oath is administered.
Badhl concept: the defendant pays money to avoid perjury, but in these claims payment is meaningless.
4
Analyze Sahibayn's dalil
The Sahibayn argue that nukul is an acknowledgment (iqrar) or its substitute. Acknowledgment is valid in all claims, including these. However, hudud and li'an are excluded because doubts (shubha) repel hudud, and li'an is analogous to hudud.
The Sahibayn's view treats nukul as a choice that forces an implied admission.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a judge following Abu Hanifa will not swear the defendant in the seven disputed cases; a judge following the Sahibayn will swear, and if the defendant refuses, judgment is given against him (nukul). Hudud and li'an are never sworn by either view.
This affects real court procedures in Hanafi jurisdictions.

اکان ادعونی ہو تو منکر سے قسم لی جائے گی یا نہیں اقوال فقہاء
قال وان كانت الدعوى نكاحا لم يستحلف المنكر عند ابي حنيفة ولا يستحلف عنده في النكاح والرجعة و الفيء في الايلاء والرق والاستيلاد والنسب والولاء والحدود واللعان وقال ابو يوسف ومحمد يستحلف فی ذالك كله الا فى الحدود واللعان وصورة الاستيلاد ان تقول الجارية انا أم ولد مولاى ولهذا ابنى منه وانكر المولى لانه لوادعى المولى ثبت الاستيلاد باقراره ولا يلتفت الى انكارها لهما ان النكول اقرار لانه يدل على كونه كاذبا في الانكار على ما قدمناه فكان اقرار وبدلا عنه والاقرار يجري في لهذه الاشياه لكنه اقرار فيه شبهة والحدود تندرىء بالشبهات واللعان فى معنى الحد ولابي حنيفة انه بذل لان معه لا يبقى اليمين واجبة لحصول المقصود وانزاله باذلا اولى كيلا يصير كاذبا فى الانكار والبذل لا يجرى في هذه الاشياء وفائدة الاستحلاف القضاء بالنكول فلا يستحلف الا ان هذا بذل لدفع الخصومة فيملكه المكاتب والعبد الماذون بمنزلة الضيافة اليسيرة وصحته في الدين بناء على زعم المدعى وهو يقيضه حقا لنفسه . والبذل معناه هناترك المنع وامر المال هين
ترجمہ اور اگر دعوی نکاح کا ہو تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک منکر سے قسم نہیں کی جائے گی اور امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک نکاح میں ، رجعت میں ، ایلاء کے رجوع کرنے میں رقیت میں ، استیلا د میں ، نسب میں ، ولاء میں ، حدود میں ، لعان میں قسم نہیں لی جائے گی اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ ان تمام میں قسم لی جائے گی سوائے حدود اور امان کے۔ اور استیلاد کی صورت یہ ہے کہ باندی کہے کہ میں اپنے مولیٰ کی ام ولد ہوں اور یہ میرا بیٹا اس سے پیدا ہوا ہے اور مولی اس سے انکار کر دے۔ اس لئے کہ اگر مولی نے دعوی کیا تو استیلا داس کے اقرار سے ثابت ہو جائے گا۔ اور باندی کے انکار کی طرف التفات نہ ہو گا ۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ قسم سے انکار کرنا ایک اقرار ہے کیونکہ مدعی علیہ کا قسم سے انکار کرنا اس بات پر دلالت کرتا ہے کہ مدعی علیہ دعوی کے انکار میں جھوٹا ہے جیسا کہ ہم نے سابق میں بیان کیا پس قسم سے انکار کرنا دعوی کا اقرار ہے یا اقرار کا بدل ہے اور ان چیزوں میں اقرار جاری ہوتا ہے۔ لیکن یہ ایسا اقرار ہے جس میں شبہ ہے اور حدود شبہات کی وجہ سے دور ہوجاتی ہیں اور اعان خد کے معنی میں ہے۔ اور امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ قسم سے انکار کرنا بذل یعنی دلیری کے ساتھ مال دینا ہے اور ہذا کیسا تھ قسم واجب نہیں رہتی ہے۔ اس لئے کہ مقصود حاصل ہو گیا اور اس کو باذل ٹھہرانا بہتر ہے تا کہ وہ انکار میں جھوٹا نہ ہو اور ان چیزوں میں بدل جاری نہیں ہوتا ہے اور قسم کا فائدہ یہ ہے کہ انکار پر حکم دے دیا جائے۔ پس قسم نہیں لی جائے گی مگر یہ بدل خصومت دور کہ لئے ہے ۔ لہذا اس کا مالک مکاتب اور عبد ماذون بھی خفیف کی ضیافت کے مرتبہ میں ہوگا اور دین میں اس کا صحیح ہونا مدعی کے گمان کی بنا پر ہے اور اس مال پر قبضہ اپنے ذاتی حق کے طور پر کرتا ہے اور اس جگہ بدل کے معنی روک کو دور کرنا ہے اور مال کا معاملہ آسان ہے۔ تشریح مصنف نے فرمایا کہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک تو چیزیں ایسی ہیں جن میں منکر یعنی مدعی علیہ سے قسم نہیں لی جاتی ہے اور و منو چیزیں یہ ہیں۔ (1) نکاح ... (2) رجعت ... (3) ایلاء ... (4) رقیت ... (5) استیلا ... (6) نسب ... (7) ولاء ... (8) حدود ... (9) لعان ... سوائے لعان کے تمام چیزیں قدوری میں مذکور ہیں اور لعان اگر چہ قدوری میں مذکور نہیں ہے لیکن جامع صغیر میں مذکور ہے۔ صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ حدود اور لعان کے علاوہ میں منکر یعنی مدعی علیہ سے قسم لی جائے گی حاصل یہ کہ حدود دلعان میں تو بالا تفاق مدعی مایه یعنی منکر سے قسم نہیں لی جائے گی لیکن باقی سات چیزوں میں اختلاف ہے یعنی امام صاحب کے نزدیک ان سات چیزوں میں قسم
Statements of jurists on whether the defendant is sworn when the claim is... He said: If the claim is marriage, the defendant is not sworn according to Abu Hanifa, and according to him, no oath is taken in marriage, return (raj'a), return in ila', slavery, istilad, lineage, wala, hadd punishments, and li'an. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Oath is taken in all that except in hudud and li'an. The form of istilad is that the slave girl says: 'I am the umm walad of my master, and this is my son from him,' and the master denies it. Because if the master claims, istilad is proven by his acknowledgment, and her denial is not considered. Their (Sahibayn's) proof is that refusal to take an oath (nukul) is an acknowledgment, because it indicates that the defendant is false in his denial, as we have explained. So it is an acknowledgment or a substitute for acknowledgment. Acknowledgment is valid in these matters, but it is an acknowledgment with doubt, and hudud are repelled by doubts, and li'an is like a hadd. Abu Hanifa's proof is that it (refusal) is bādhil (payment), because with it the oath is no longer obligatory since the objective is achieved, and it is better to consider him a payor (bādhil) so that he does not become a liar in his denial. Payment (bādhil) is not valid in these matters. The benefit of swearing is to give judgment based on nukul, so no oath is taken. Rather, this is a payment to dismiss the litigation, so a mukatib and an authorized slave may possess it, similar to light hospitality. Its validity in debt is based on the claimant's assumption, and he takes possession of it as his own right. The meaning of bādhil here is to remove the prohibition, and the matter of wealth is easy. Translation: And if the claim is marriage, according to Abu Hanifa the defendant is not sworn, and according to him, no oath is taken in marriage, raj'a, return in ila', riqq, istilad, nasab, wala', hudud, and li'an. The Sahibayn said: Oath is taken in all of that except hudud and li'an. The case of istilad is that the slave girl says: 'I am my master's umm walad and this is my son from him,' and the master denies it. Because if the master claims, istilad is proven by his acknowledgment, and her denial is not considered. The Sahibayn's proof is that refusal to swear is an acknowledgment, because the defendant's refusal to swear indicates he is false in his denial, as we have previously explained. So it is an acknowledgment or a substitute for acknowledgment. Acknowledgment is valid in these things, but it is an acknowledgment with doubt, and hudud are repelled by doubts, and li'an is like a hadd. Abu Hanifa's proof is that refusal to swear is a payment (bādhil), in the sense of giving money to end the claim, and with it the oath is no longer obligatory because the objective is achieved. It is better to consider him a payor so that he does not become a liar in his denial. Payment does not apply in these matters. The benefit of swearing is to give judgment based on nukul, so the oath is not taken unless this is a payment to dismiss the litigation. Therefore, a mukatib and an authorized slave may possess it, similar to light hospitality. Its validity in debt is based on the claimant's assumption, and he takes possession of it as his own right. In this context, bādhil means to remove prohibition, and the matter of wealth is easy. Explanation: The author said that according to Abu Hanifa, there are matters in which the defendant is not sworn, and those matters are: (1) marriage, (2) raj'a, (3) ila', (4) slavery, (5) istilad, (6) lineage, (7) wala, (8) hudud, (9) li'an. All except li'an are mentioned in al-Quduri; li'an is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir. The Sahibayn said: Oath is taken from the defendant except in hudud and li'an. The summary: In hudud and li'an, by agreement, the defendant is not sworn. But in the remaining seven, there is disagreement: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, in these seven matters, oath is not taken (the text cuts off).
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses the ruling on whether the defendant (munkir) is required to take an oath in specific types of claims. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, no oath is taken from the defendant in nine cases: marriage, raj'a (return during iddah after revocable divorce), fay' (return in ila'), riqq (slavery), istilad (claim of a slave girl that she is umm walad), nasab (lineage), wala (clientage), hudud (fixed punishments), and li'an (mutual imprecation). Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree: they hold that an oath is taken in all these cases except hudud and li'an. The passage provides the proofs for both positions. The Sahibayn argue that refusing the oath (nukul) constitutes an acknowledgment (iqrar) or its substitute, and acknowledgment is valid in these matters. However, they admit that this acknowledgment is subject to doubt (shubha), and since hudud are repelled by doubts, they are excluded. Li'an is treated like hudud. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the defendant's refusal to swear is considered a bādhil (payment to end litigation), which is a payment of money or property. According to him, such payment is not applicable in these matters because they involve personal status, lineage, or rights of God, not pure financial transactions. Therefore, no oath is taken. The passage then enumerates the nine cases and clarifies the source of each ruling. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that in these seven matters (excluding hudud and li'an which are agreed upon), Abu Hanifa does not administer an oath, while the Sahibayn do. The text concludes that the disagreement is over these seven cases.

Key Terms
استحلاف (istihlāf): The act of requiring the defendant to take an oath to deny the claim.
نكول (nukūl): Refusal of the defendant to take the oath when required.
بذل (badhl): Payment made by the defendant to end the litigation, avoiding the oath.
مدعى عليه (mudda'ā 'alayhi): The defendant against whom a claim is made.
منكر (munkir): The defendant who denies the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following cases do the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree with Abu Hanifa that the defendant should not be sworn?
A) Marriage and raj'a
B) Istilad and lineage
C) Hudud and li'an
D) Slavery and wala

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, Abu Hanifa prohibits swearing the defendant in nine specific cases, while the Sahibayn permit it except in hudud and li'an, based on different theories of nukul as payment or acknowledgment.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
15 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Oath in Seven Claims
Intra-Hanafi dispute on taking oath in seven specific claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the oath in seven specific claims and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Refusal to swear an oath can mean admission or boldness; the ambiguity blocks the judge's ruling.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a defendant must swear an oath in claims involving marriage, divorce revocation, and lineage. While hudud and li'an are agreed exceptions, the remaining seven categories are contested between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two distinguished students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi approach to procedural evidence and the weight given to doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and agreed exceptions
The core issue is whether the defendant (munkir) is required to take an oath in certain claims. Hudud (fixed penalties) and li'an (mutual cursing) are agreed exceptions where no oath is taken.
These exceptions are based on the principle that doubts (shubuhat) ward off fixed penalties.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that no oath is taken in the seven claims: nikah, raj'ah, ila', riqqiyyah, istila', nasab, wala'. If the plaintiff has no clear evidence, the case is dismissed.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn argue that refusal to swear is strong evidence of falsehood, because a truthful person would welcome the benefits of swearing. Therefore, refusal is treated as admission (iqrar), and the judge rules against the defendant.
Benefits cited: fulfilling obligation, removing false accusation, retaining property, gaining reward.
4
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's counter-argument
Imam Abu Hanifa argues that refusal could also be due to badal (boldness to give up the claimed property), which also removes the dispute. Since refusal is ambiguous, it cannot be taken as conclusive admission.
The author of Al-Hidayah supports this ambiguity argument.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the defendant refuses the oath, the judge cannot rule in favor of the plaintiff; the case is simply dismissed. According to the Sahibayn, the judge may rule against the defendant based on refusal.
The mufta bihi (operative position) is that of Imam Abu Hanifa.

(۵) استیلا یعنی ایک باندی نے اپنے مولی پر دعوی کیا کہ میں نے اس کے نطفہ سے ایک بچہ جنا اور مولیٰ نے اس کا انکار کیا۔ اس صورت میں عکس جاری نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ اگر مولی نے دعوی کیا تو مولی کے اقرار سے استیلا دثابت ہو جائے گا اور باندی کے انکار کی طرف التفات نہ ہو گا ۔

Book of Claims. Effect in Al-Bidayyah volume - and asceticism. The substitute is not valid and the benefit of the oath is that a ruling is given upon denial. So the oath will not be taken, but this is a substitute for removing the dispute. Therefore, its owner, a mukatib and a ma'dhun slave, will also be in the rank of a modest hospitality. Its correctness in religion is based on the plaintiff's assumption, and he takes possession of this property as his own right. Here the meaning of 'badal' is to remove the obstacle, and the matter of property is easy. The author explained that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, there are things in which the denier, i.e., the defendant, is not required to take an oath, and those things are: (1) Marriage: a man claims he married a woman and she denies, or a woman claims marriage and the man denies. (2) Raj'ah: a man gave a revocable divorce and the iddah ended, then he claims he took her back during iddah but she denies, or she claims he took her back and he denies. (3) Ila': after the period of ila' passes, the man claims he returned to her during the ila' period and she denies, or she claims return and he denies. (4) Slavery: a person claims an unknown lineage person is his slave and he denies, or the unknown claims he is his slave and he denies. (5) Istila': a slave-woman claims she gave birth to a child from her master's sperm and he denies. (6) Lineage: a person claims an unknown is his son or father and he denies, or vice versa. (7) Wala': a person claims he freed an unknown and is his master, and he denies, or vice versa. (8) Hudud: a person claims something against another that necessitates a fixed penalty, like zina or theft, and he denies. (9) Li'an: a woman claims her husband accused her of adultery, and he denies. Except for li'an, all are mentioned in Al-Quduri; li'an is in Al-Jami' al-Saghir. The Sahibayn said: except for hudud and li'an, the defendant is required to take an oath. So hudud and li'an are agreed upon: no oath. The remaining seven are disputed: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, no oath in these seven; according to the two companions, oath is taken. The meaning of not taking oath according to Imam Abu Hanifa is that if the plaintiff has no clear evidence, the case will be dismissed and the judge cannot decide in his favor. The proof of the two companions is that the defendant's refusal to swear an oath is a kind of admission of the plaintiff's claim, because refusing to swear indicates that the defendant is absolutely lying in his denial. If he were not lying, he would take the oath because there are three benefits: fulfilling the obligation of the oath, removing the harm of false accusation, and keeping the property in his possession; a fourth benefit is obtaining reward by uttering Allah's name. Since he rejects these benefits, it is clear proof he is lying. Thus, refusal is admission or its replacement, and admission is valid in all things. Therefore, refusal to swear is also valid in all things, and refusal is only valid when the defendant is asked to swear. So it is established that in these cases the defendant is asked to swear. Also, refusal is admission, and admission is valid in these cases; thus refusal is valid, and when refusal is valid, the benefit of the judge's decision exists, so the defendant is asked to swear. However, refusal is an admission with doubt, because it is silence, and silence is admission but with doubt. Hudud are avoided due to doubts. Therefore, with this doubtful admission, hadd is not imposed; refusal is not valid for hadd, so the judge's decision benefit is not established, hence no oath. In li'an, it is like hadd: li'an stands in place of hadd for the husband (for slander) and for the woman (for zina). So in li'an, refusal is not valid and no oath. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the defendant's refusal is badal (meaning boldness to give), and the purpose of the oath is to remove the dispute, and with bdhal the dispute is also removed, so the oath is not obligatory. The author of Al-Hidayah says on behalf of Imam Abu Hanifa that the defendant's refusal to swear carries two possibilities: one is admission, as the Sahibayn say; the other is bdhal, i.e., intention to give the property with boldness and courage, as Imam Abu Hanifa said.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah deals with an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement of an oath (yamin) from the defendant (munkir) in certain types of claims. The author first lists nine categories where the issue arises: (1) nikah (marriage claim), (2) raj'ah (revocation of divorce), (3) ila' (vow of abstinence), (4) riqqiyyah (claim of slavery), (5) istila' (claim of giving birth to master's child), (6) nasab (lineage), (7) wala' (clientage of emancipation), (8) hudud (fixed penalties), and (9) li'an (mutual cursing). It is agreed upon (bil-ittifaq) that in hudud and li'an, no oath is taken from the defendant. The dispute is about the remaining seven categories. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that in these seven, the defendant is not asked to take an oath; if the plaintiff has no clear proof, the case is dismissed. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the defendant is asked to take an oath, and if he refuses, it is treated as an admission (iqrar) of the claim. The passage then explains the reasoning of each side. The Sahibayn argue that refusal to swear is strong evidence of falsehood because a truthful person would welcome the benefits of swearing. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that refusal could be due to bdhal (a bold intention to give what is claimed) which also removes the dispute, making the oath unnecessary. The author of al-Hidayah supports Imam Abu Hanifa's view by noting that refusal has two possible interpretations (admission or boldness), and thus the oath cannot be demanded. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the judge cannot rule in favor of the plaintiff if the defendant refuses the oath; the case is simply dismissed. According to the Sahibayn, the judge can rule against the defendant based on refusal. The passage ends by clarifying that in hudud and li'an, even the Sahibayn agree that no oath is taken because of the principle that doubts (shubuhat) ward off fixed penalties.

Key Terms
دعوی (da'wa): A legal claim or assertion made by one party (plaintiff) against another (defendant).
یمین (yamin): An oath sworn by the defendant to deny the claim, required in certain cases to resolve the dispute.
انکار (inkar): The defendant's denial of the plaintiff's claim.
اقرار (iqrar): An admission or confession of the claim by the defendant, which is binding and allows the judge to rule against him.
بدل (badal): Boldness or willingness to give the claimed property voluntarily, which removes the need for an oath.
حد (hadd): Fixed penal sanction prescribed by Shari'ah for specific crimes like zina, theft, etc. These are warded off by doubts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following claims is NOT among the seven disputed claims regarding the defendant's oath?
A) Nikah (marriage)
B) Raj'ah (revocation of divorce)
C) Hudud (fixed penalties)
D) Nasab (lineage)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on whether the defendant must swear in seven specific claims; the relied-upon view is that no oath is required due to the ambiguity of refusal.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
10 min
The Legal Effect of Oath Refusal: Admission or Waiver?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on legal effect of oath refusal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the legal effect of a defendant's refusal to take an oath and explain the practical consequence of each view.

Core Idea
A refusal to swear is either an admission (Sahibayn) or a waiver (Abu Hanifa) — two rulings, one procedure.

When a defendant refuses to take a demanded oath, what does that mean? This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah reveals a critical division within the Hanafi school. Imam Abu Hanifa views the refusal as a waiver (badl) — the defendant is ready to settle. The Sahibayn see it as an admission (iqrar). Understanding this dispute is essential for anyone involved in Islamic litigation, as it determines whether the judge can compel the oath and rule against the defendant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State Imam Abu Hanifa's view (refusal is badl/waiver) and the Sahibayn's view (refusal is iqrar/admission).
The passage explicitly contrasts these two positions.
2
Follow each argument
Analyze the Sahibayn's rational argument: a rational person would not forfeit the benefits of an oath unless lying. Then analyze Abu Hanifa's argument: refusal may be due to willingness to settle, and interpreting it as admission would wrongfully label a Muslim a liar.
Both arguments are talil (rational reasoning).
3
Note the exception
Both views agree that in hudud (fixed punishments) and li'an (mutual cursing) cases, the refusal does not lead to a ruling against the defendant because doubts avert hudud.
This shows the scope of the ikhtilaf.
4
Apply the tarjih
The commentator (Ashraf al-Hidayah) prefers Imam Abu Hanifa's view as it protects a Muslim from the accusation of falsehood. This is the mufta bihi (operative) position.
Remember that the Sahibayn's view is also valid but not relied upon for fatwa.

جاری ہوگا اور انکار عن الیمین اسی وقت جاری ہوگا جبکہ مدعی علیہ سے قسم لی جائے گی۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ مذکورہ مسائل میں مدعلی علیہ سے قسم لی جائے گی اس کو شکل اول کے طور پر مختصر ایوں بھی کہہ سکتے ہیں کہ
